Locations of Internal Powers in the Brain
Al-Iji assigns each internal power to a specific brain ventricle and proves this by the effect of lesions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how medieval Muslim theologians localized internal psychological powers in the brain using anatomical observation and lesion evidence.

Core Idea
Each internal power resides in a distinct brain ventricle, as proven by lesion studies.

In this passage, al-Iji presents the empirical grounding for the internal powers of the soul. Drawing on Galenic anatomy, he assigns each power—common sense, imagination, estimative, retentive, and imaginative—to a specific brain ventricle. The proof lies in the observed effect of brain lesions: damage to a certain area impairs the corresponding power alone, demonstrating localization.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall structure.
Notice the anatomical terms like 'first ventricle' and 'worm'.
2
Identify the five internal powers
The passage mentions: common sense, imagination, estimative, retentive, and imaginative (compositive). List them and note their functions as described.
Common sense receives sensibles; imagination preserves them; etc.
3
Map each power to a ventricle location
Al-Iji assigns each power to a specific part of the brain: front of first ventricle, back of first ventricle, front of last ventricle, back of last ventricle, and the worm. Make a chart.
The imaginative power in the worm combines and separates forms and meanings.
4
Understand the proof by lesions
The passage states that the loci were known by 'al-afa' (lesion): when a specific area is affected, only that power's function is disrupted. This shows cause-and-effect localization.
This is a rudimentary form of clinical reasoning.
5
Reflect on the significance
This approach shows that Islamic theologians used empirical observation to support metaphysical doctrines about the soul's faculties.
It bridges philosophy and medicine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : محل الحس المشترك والخيال ) هو ( البطن الأول من الدماغ ) المنقسم إلى بطون ثلاثة أعظمها الأول ثم الثالث وأما الثاني فهو كمنفذ فيما بينهما منفرد على شكل الدودة ) فالحس المشترك في مقدمه ) أي مقدم البطن الأول لتصادفه المحسوسات) بالحواس الظاهرة (أولاً) والخيال في مؤخره ) لأنه خزانتها التي تحفظها ) ومحل الوهمية والحافظة) هو ( البطن الأخير منه والوهمية في مقدمه والحافظة في مؤخره على قياس حال الحس المشترك والخيال في البطن الأول ( ومحل المتخيلة) هو ( الدودة الحاصلة في وسط الدماغ الموضوعة بين البطنين من هذه المحسوسات التي في أحد جانبيها ( و ) من ( هذه المعاني الجزئية التي في الجانب الآخر ( فتتصرف بالتركيب والتفصيل (فيما فيهما) أي في البطنين الأول والأخير من الصور والمعاني والمشهور في الكتب المعول عليها أن المتخيلة في مقدم الدودة والوهمية في مؤخرها والحافظة في مقدم البطن الأخير وليس في مؤخره شيء من هذه القوى إذ لا حارس هناك من الحواس فتكثر مصادماته المؤدية إلى الاختلال ( وإنما عرف محالها المذكورة ( بالآفة فإنه إذا تطرق آفة إلى محل من هذه المحال اختل فعل القوة المخصوصة به دون غيرها أي دون فعل غيرها من أفعال سائر القوى . ( ولولا اختصاص كل من هذه القوى ) بمحله لما كان ) الأمر كذلك
The second (investigation): The locus of the common sense and the imagination is the first ventricle of the brain, which is divided into three ventricles, the largest of which is the first, then the third; as for the second, it is a passage between them, solitary in the form of a worm. So the common sense is in its front, i.e., the front of the first ventricle, because it receives the sensibles by the external senses first, and the imagination is in its back, because it is its treasury that preserves them. And the locus of the estimative power and the retentive power is the last ventricle of it, with the estimative power in its front and the retentive power in its back, analogous to the state of the common sense and the imagination in the first ventricle. And the locus of the imaginative power is the worm located in the middle of the brain, placed between the two ventricles, from these sensibles which are on one side and from these particular meanings which are on the other side, so it disposes by combination and separation in what is in them, i.e., in the first and last ventricles, of forms and meanings. And the well-known in the authoritative books is that the imaginative power is in the front of the worm, the estimative power in its back, and the retentive power in the front of the last ventricle, and there is nothing of these powers in its back, because there is no guard (haris) there from the senses, so its collisions (musadamatu) would increase, leading to disorder. And they only knew their mentioned loci by affliction (al-afa), for if an affliction affects a locus from these loci, the act of the particular power is corrupted without others, i.e., without the act of other powers. And were it not for the specificity of each of these powers to its locus, it would not be so.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents the second investigation concerning the physical loci of the internal powers in the brain. It begins by stating the locus of common sense (hiss mushtarak) and imagination (khayal) as the first ventricle (batn) of the brain, which is divided into three ventricles; the first is largest, then the third, while the second acts as a passage in the shape of a worm. The common sense is in the front of the first ventricle because it receives sensibles from the external senses first, while the imagination is in the back because it preserves them. The locus of the estimative power and retentive power is the last (third) ventricle, with the estimative in front and retentive in back, analogous to the arrangement in the first ventricle. The locus of the imaginative power is the worm (dooda) in the middle of the brain, positioned between the two ventricles. It disposes in the sensibles from one side and particular meanings from the other, combining and separating them. The author then notes that in authoritative books, the commonly accepted arrangement is that the imaginative power is in the front of the worm, the estimative in its back, and the retentive in the front of the last ventricle, with nothing in its back because there is no guard (haris) from the senses. The reasoning for these loci is based on lesions (afa): if a specific area is damaged, only the corresponding function is impaired, indicating localization. Without such specificity, that would not be the case.

Key Terms
الحس المشترك
الخيال
البطن الأول
الدودة
الآفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All internal powers are located in the same region of the brain.
Correction: The passage shows each power has a distinct location, and damage to one region only disrupts its corresponding power.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what evidence is used to determine the loci of the internal powers in the brain?
A) Inspiration from God.
B) The effect of lesions (al-afa) on specific functions.
C) The consensus of physicians.
D) Metaphysical reasoning alone.

Key Takeaway
The localization of internal powers in the brain is known empirically through the observation that lesions impair specific functions, not through pure speculation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that a physical lesion to a specific part of your brain can corrupt the power of estimation (wahmiyya) – the very power by which you judge spiritual dangers. How does this fragility of your inner capacities make you feel about the health of your brain? What are you doing today to protect it for the sake of your faith?
?
Al-Iji proves the location of these powers through lesions: damage to a locus corrupts that power alone. Have you ever experienced a time when a weakness – whether physical, emotional, or spiritual – specifically affected one of your inner faculties (like your ability to imagine, remember, or judge)? What did that reveal to you about the connection between your body and your soul, and what change does it call for in your life?
?
If it is true that your common sense (hiss mushtarak) is located in the front of the first ventricle, and that it can be impaired by a lesion, how does that affect your gratitude for your current ability to perceive truth? When you reflect on the warnings of the Qur'an about those who have hearts but do not understand, eyes but do not see – could a physical affliction be a reminder of a spiritual one? What will you do to safeguard your spiritual perception today?

The Soul as the Single Perceiver of All Perceptions
Al-Iji presents three proofs that the rational soul is the perceiver of all perceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational proofs that the soul, not multiple faculties, is the single perceiver of all perceptions.

Core Idea
The soul is one, and it alone perceives all universals and particulars.

In this passage, Al-Iji concludes his discussion on the internal powers by arguing that the rational soul is the sole perceiver of all kinds of perceptions. He provides three proofs: one based on the judgment of universals over particulars, one from self-experience, and one from the soul's management of the body. Let's examine each.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once through in Arabic, then the English translation. Let the author's own argument unfold before analysis.
Notice the structure: a preamble stating the background, then three numbered proofs.
2
Understand the first proof (judgment of universals)
Reflect on how we say 'Zayd is human' (universal on particular) and 'Zayd is not 'Amr' (particular is not another). Such judgments require a power that knows both universals and all particulars, and that power cannot be corporeal. It is the rational soul.
This proof relies on the incompatibility of corporeal faculties with universal judgments.
3
Understand the second proof (experiential)
Consider your own experience: you find yourself hearing, seeing, feeling hungry, and thinking. The perceiver of all these diverse perceptions is one 'I'. This is an undeniable self-evident truth.
This is a direct appeal to immediate self-awareness.
4
Understand the third proof (management of the body)
The soul manages the body and performs particular actions. As the agent, it must perceive these particulars, further showing it is the single perceiver.
This proof ties perception to action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة لأبحاث النوع الثاني وهي البحث الثالث: أكثر الكلام الذي نقلناه عنهم ( في ) إثبات ( هذه القوى ) وتعددها ( بعد ) بنائه على (نفي القادر المختار) الموجد لجميع الأشياء ابتداء بمجرد إرادته مبني ) على أن النفس ) الناطقة (ليست مدركة للجزئيات كما أشرنا إليه في أثناء الكلام المنقول (فلنتكلم في ذلك فنقول : المدرك لجميع أصناف الإدراكات ) هو ( النفس لوجوه : الأول : ما ذكرناه من الحكم بالكلي على الجزئي ) في مثل قولنا : زيد إنسان ) وبكل جزئي على أنه غير الآخر) أي والحكم بسلب أحد الجزأين عن الآخر كما في قولك : زيد ليس بعمرو، فلا بد من قوة تدرك الكليات وجميع أنواع الجزئيات من المحسوسات مشاهدة ومتخيلة والمعاني الجزئية متوهمة ومحفوظة ولا يجوز أن تكون هذه القوى جسمانية اتفاقاً فهي القوة العاقلة ( الثاني : وجداني بلا شبهة (إني واجد أسمع وأبصر وأجوع وأشبع وأدرك المعقولات فالمدرك للكل واحد وليس إلا النفس. ( الثالث : أن النفس مدبرة للبدن ( المعين ( فهو ) أي النفس بتأويل الإنسان (فاعل للجزئيات ) من الأفعال التدبيرية ( ولا بد له فيه أي في كونه فاعلاً للأفعال الجزئية
Conclusion of the investigations of the second type, and it is the third investigation: Most of the speech we transmitted from them concerning the proof of these powers and their multiplicity, after its construction upon the negation of the Omnipotent Agent (al-qadir al-mukhtar) who creates all things ex nihilo by His mere will, is built upon the fact that the rational soul (al-nafs al-natiqa) is not a perceiver of particulars, as we indicated in the course of the transmitted speech. So let us speak about that and say: The perceiver of all kinds of perceptions is the soul, for reasons: First: What we have mentioned of the judgment of the universal upon the particular, like our saying: Zayd is a human, and of every particular that it is not the other, i.e., the judgment of negating one of the two parts from the other, as in your saying: Zayd is not 'Amr. So there must be a power that perceives universals and all kinds of particulars, from sensibles, observed and imagined, and particular meanings, estimated and preserved. And it is not permissible for these powers to be corporeal by agreement; so it is the rational power. Second: It is experiential without doubt: I find myself hearing, seeing, becoming hungry, becoming full, and perceiving intelligibles. So the perceiver of all is one and it is none but the soul. Third: That the soul is the manager of the particular body, so it (i.e., the soul interpreted as the human) is the agent of particulars from the managerial actions, and it is necessary for it in being the agent of partial actions...
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion (khatima) of the investigations of the second type, specifically the third investigation. The author notes that most of the previously transmitted discourse on proving the multiplicity of internal powers was based on denying the All-Powerful Agent (God) and assuming that the rational soul does not perceive particulars. Here, he intends to argue that the soul is the perceiver of all kinds of perceptions. He gives three proofs. First, the judgment of universals on particulars (e.g., 'Zayd is human') and the judgment that each particular is not another (e.g., 'Zayd is not 'Amr') require a power that perceives universals and all types of particulars (sensory, imagined, estimated, preserved). Such a power cannot be corporeal by universal agreement; it is the rational power (al-quwwa al-aqila). Second, an experiential proof: one finds oneself hearing, seeing, feeling hungry, full, and perceiving intelligibles; the perceiver of all these is one and the same, namely the soul. Third, the soul is the manager of the body and is the agent of particular managerial actions; as the agent of such actions, it must perceive the particulars involved. This third proof is incomplete in the excerpt.

Key Terms
النفس
المدرك
الكلي
الجزئي
القوة العاقلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that separate brain powers perceive different things (e.g., eyes see, ears hear), and that there is no single perceiver.
Correction: The soul is the single perceiver that experiences all perceptions, as shown by immediate self-awareness and the need for a non-corporeal power to make universal judgments.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that the soul is the perceiver of all perceptions?
A) The soul manages the body.
B) The judgment of universals on particulars requires a non-corporeal power.
C) The external senses need a single receiver.
D) The brain is too limited to contain multiple powers.

Key Takeaway
The soul is the single perceiver of all perceptions because only a non-corporeal substance can judge universals and particulars together, as shown by logic, experience, and its management of the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear, see, feel hungry, or think, do you ever pause to realize that it is the same 'you' experiencing all of these? How often do you live as if you are a collection of separate experiences rather than a unified soul? Commit today to remember, in every sensation, that it is your soul that perceives—and that it will be accountable for what it has witnessed.
?
The second proof says you find yourself hearing and seeing. But have you ever truly listened to your own inner witness while sinning? Imagine the moment you engage in a forbidden act—are you aware that your soul is present, perceiving it all? If you were fully conscious of the soul's constant perception, would you still commit that sin? Why not start now, by asking your soul to be present in your next decision?
?
Consider the logical proof: you judge that a person is human and that he is not another. This simple act requires a single intellect. But how often do you use this rational power to judge yourself rather than others? Does your soul ever judge your own actions with the same clarity as you distinguish Zayd from 'Amr? This very ability to discern truth is a gift; use it tonight to examine one habit you've ignored, and let your soul be the judge.

Common Sense and Imagination in the First Ventricle
Sanusi locates the common sense and imagination in the first ventricle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the common sense and imagination are located in the front and rear of the first ventricle of the brain, respectively, and their functional roles.

Core Idea
Common sense receives; imagination stores.

Sanusi begins the second point of his discussion by detailing the internal perceptive powers. He first focuses on the common sense and imagination, assigning them specific loci in the first ventricle of the brain. This lesson explores that assignment and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to understand Sanusi's description of the first ventricle and the placement of common sense and imagination.
Notice the anatomical description of the three ventricles.
2
Identify the Two Powers
Identify the two powers discussed: common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) and imagination (al-khayāl). Note their respective locations in the first ventricle.
Common sense is in the front, imagination in the rear.
3
Understand the Reasoning
Understand why common sense is at the front (it first encounters sensory objects) and imagination at the rear (it is the storehouse).
4
Connect to the Overall Lesson
Reflect on how this assignment fits into the broader system of internal powers and their physical loci.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : محل الحس المشترك والخيال ) هو ( البطن الأول من الدماغ ) المنقسم إلى بطون ثلاثة أعظمها الأول ثم الثالث وأما الثاني فهو كمنفذ فيما بينهما منفرد على شكل الدودة ) فالحس المشترك في مقدمه ) أي مقدم البطن الأول لتصادفه المحسوسات) بالحواس الظاهرة (أولاً) والخيال في مؤخره ) لأنه خزانتها التي تحفظها ) ومحل الوهمية والحافظة) هو ( البطن الأخير منه والوهمية في مقدمه والحافظة في مؤخره على قياس حال الحس المشترك والخيال في البطن الأول ( ومحل المتخيلة) هو ( الدودة الحاصلة في وسط الدماغ الموضوعة بين البطنين من هذه المحسوسات التي في أحد جانبيها ( و ) من ( هذه المعاني الجزئية التي في الجانب الآخر ( فتتصرف بالتركيب والتفصيل (فيما فيهما) أي في البطنين الأول والأخير من الصور والمعاني والمشهور في الكتب المعول عليها أن المتخيلة في مقدم الدودة والوهمية في مؤخرها والحافظة في مقدم البطن الأخير وليس في مؤخره شيء من هذه القوى إذ لا حارس هناك من الحواس فتكثر مصادماته المؤدية إلى الاختلال ( وإنما عرف محالها المذكورة ( بالآفة فإنه إذا تطرق آفة إلى محل من هذه المحال اختل فعل القوة المخصوصة به دون غيرها أي دون فعل غيرها من أفعال سائر القوى . ( ولولا اختصاص كل من هذه القوى ) بمحله لما كان ) الأمر كذلك
The second: The locus of the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) and imagination (al-khayāl) is the first ventricle of the brain, which is divided into three ventricles, the largest of which is the first, then the third; as for the second, it is like a passage between them, isolated in the shape of a worm. So the common sense is in its front, i.e., the front of the first ventricle, because it first encounters the objects perceived by the external senses; and imagination is in its rear, because it is its storehouse that preserves them. And the locus of the estimative faculty (al-wahmiyya) and the memory (al-ḥāfiẓa) is the last ventricle of it; the estimative faculty in its front and the memory in its rear, on the analogy of the case of the common sense and imagination in the first ventricle. And the locus of the compositive imagination (al-mutakhayyila) is the worm-like structure located in the middle of the brain, placed between the two ventricles, from these sensory images which are on one side of it, and from these particular meanings which are on the other side; so it operates by composition and division concerning what is in them, i.e., in the first and last ventricles, of forms and meanings. And what is well-known in the reliable books is that the compositive imagination is in the front of the worm, the estimative faculty in its rear, and the memory in the front of the last ventricle, and there is none of these powers in its rear, because there is no guardian there from the senses, so its collisions that lead to disorder increase. And the aforementioned loci are only known by lesion, for if a lesion afflicts a locus among these loci, the action of the power particular to it is disordered without others, i.e., without the action of other actions of the remaining powers. And were it not for the specialization of each of these powers to its locus, it would not be so.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a classification and description of the internal perceptive powers and their anatomical locations in the brain. The author specifies the second point (الثاني) in a series, which is the locus (محل) of each power. The brain is described as having three ventricles (بطون), the largest being the first and the third, with the second acting as a passage shaped like a worm (دودة). The common sense (الحس المشترك) is at the front of the first ventricle because it first receives sensory data, while imagination (الخيال) is at the rear as its storehouse. The last ventricle contains the estimative faculty (الوهمية) at the front and memory (الحافظة) at the rear, analogous to the first ventricle. The compositive imagination (المتخيلة) is in the worm-like structure between the two ventricles, combining and separating sensory images and meanings. A popular view relocates some powers within the worm and last ventricle. The loci are known by lesion (الآفة): if a lesion occurs in a locus, the specific power is disrupted, proving specialization. Otherwise, without such specialization, the effects would not be localized.

Key Terms
الحس المشترك
الخيال
البطن الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think these powers are not physically localized or are located in the heart.
Correction: The text explicitly locates them in the brain's first ventricle, emphasizing their physical basis.

Checkpoint
According to the text, which internal power is located in the front of the first ventricle of the brain?
A) Common sense
B) Imagination
C) Estimative faculty
D) Memory

Key Takeaway
The common sense is in the front of the first ventricle to receive sensory data, and imagination is in the rear to store it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The common sense receives objects from the external senses, and imagination stores them. In your daily life, which faculty do you neglect more—receiving beneficial knowledge or preserving it? What one thing will you intentionally store in your imagination today from what you see or hear?
?
The first ventricle is the largest of the three. Allah gave you a mind with great capacity. Are you using your mental faculties to seek what pleases Him, or are you filling them with distractions? What specific change will you make today to prioritize divine knowledge over vain thoughts?

Estimative Faculty, Memory, and Compositive Imagination
Sanusi assigns the estimative faculty and memory to the last ventricle, and the compositive imagination to the worm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the loci of the estimative faculty, memory, and compositive imagination in the brain, including the well-known alternative assignment.

Core Idea
Each internal power has a distinct physical home in the brain.

Continuing his enumeration of internal perceptive powers, Sanusi now turns to the remaining three: the estimative faculty, memory, and compositive imagination. He assigns them to specific parts of the brain, noting both his own view and a well-known alternative from reliable books. This lesson unpacks these assignments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation, focusing on the locations of the estimative faculty, memory, and compositive imagination.
Pay attention to the 'worm' structure and its role.
2
Map the Powers
Identify which power is in which part: estimative faculty and memory in the last ventricle, compositive imagination in the worm.
Note the analogy with the first ventricle.
3
Examine the Well-Known View
Consider the alternative assignment from reliable books: compositive imagination in front of worm, estimative faculty in rear of worm, memory in front of last ventricle.
4
Understand the Composition Function
Understand that the compositive imagination combines and separates sensory images and meanings from both ventricles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : محل الحس المشترك والخيال ) هو ( البطن الأول من الدماغ ) المنقسم إلى بطون ثلاثة أعظمها الأول ثم الثالث وأما الثاني فهو كمنفذ فيما بينهما منفرد على شكل الدودة ) فالحس المشترك في مقدمه ) أي مقدم البطن الأول لتصادفه المحسوسات) بالحواس الظاهرة (أولاً) والخيال في مؤخره ) لأنه خزانتها التي تحفظها ) ومحل الوهمية والحافظة) هو ( البطن الأخير منه والوهمية في مقدمه والحافظة في مؤخره على قياس حال الحس المشترك والخيال في البطن الأول ( ومحل المتخيلة) هو ( الدودة الحاصلة في وسط الدماغ الموضوعة بين البطنين من هذه المحسوسات التي في أحد جانبيها ( و ) من ( هذه المعاني الجزئية التي في الجانب الآخر ( فتتصرف بالتركيب والتفصيل (فيما فيهما) أي في البطنين الأول والأخير من الصور والمعاني والمشهور في الكتب المعول عليها أن المتخيلة في مقدم الدودة والوهمية في مؤخرها والحافظة في مقدم البطن الأخير وليس في مؤخره شيء من هذه القوى إذ لا حارس هناك من الحواس فتكثر مصادماته المؤدية إلى الاختلال ( وإنما عرف محالها المذكورة ( بالآفة فإنه إذا تطرق آفة إلى محل من هذه المحال اختل فعل القوة المخصوصة به دون غيرها أي دون فعل غيرها من أفعال سائر القوى . ( ولولا اختصاص كل من هذه القوى ) بمحله لما كان ) الأمر كذلك
The second: The locus of the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) and imagination (al-khayāl) is the first ventricle of the brain, which is divided into three ventricles, the largest of which is the first, then the third; as for the second, it is like a passage between them, isolated in the shape of a worm. So the common sense is in its front, i.e., the front of the first ventricle, because it first encounters the objects perceived by the external senses; and imagination is in its rear, because it is its storehouse that preserves them. And the locus of the estimative faculty (al-wahmiyya) and the memory (al-ḥāfiẓa) is the last ventricle of it; the estimative faculty in its front and the memory in its rear, on the analogy of the case of the common sense and imagination in the first ventricle. And the locus of the compositive imagination (al-mutakhayyila) is the worm-like structure located in the middle of the brain, placed between the two ventricles, from these sensory images which are on one side of it, and from these particular meanings which are on the other side; so it operates by composition and division concerning what is in them, i.e., in the first and last ventricles, of forms and meanings. And what is well-known in the reliable books is that the compositive imagination is in the front of the worm, the estimative faculty in its rear, and the memory in the front of the last ventricle, and there is none of these powers in its rear, because there is no guardian there from the senses, so its collisions that lead to disorder increase. And the aforementioned loci are only known by lesion, for if a lesion afflicts a locus among these loci, the action of the power particular to it is disordered without others, i.e., without the action of other actions of the remaining powers. And were it not for the specialization of each of these powers to its locus, it would not be so.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a classification and description of the internal perceptive powers and their anatomical locations in the brain. The author specifies the second point (الثاني) in a series, which is the locus (محل) of each power. The brain is described as having three ventricles (بطون), the largest being the first and the third, with the second acting as a passage shaped like a worm (دودة). The common sense (الحس المشترك) is at the front of the first ventricle because it first receives sensory data, while imagination (الخيال) is at the rear as its storehouse. The last ventricle contains the estimative faculty (الوهمية) at the front and memory (الحافظة) at the rear, analogous to the first ventricle. The compositive imagination (المتخيلة) is in the worm-like structure between the two ventricles, combining and separating sensory images and meanings. A popular view relocates some powers within the worm and last ventricle. The loci are known by lesion (الآفة): if a lesion occurs in a locus, the specific power is disrupted, proving specialization. Otherwise, without such specialization, the effects would not be localized.

Key Terms
الوهمية
الحافظة
المتخيلة
الدودة
البطن الأخير

B) In the rear of the last ventricle
C) In the front of the worm
D) In the front of the last ventricle

Key Takeaway
The estimative faculty and memory are in the last ventricle, while the compositive imagination resides in the worm; a well-known view slightly relocates some of these.

The Method of Lesion in Determining Loci
Sanusi explains that the loci are known by lesion, proving specialization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the loci of internal perceptive powers are known through the method of lesion, and why this supports their specialization.

Core Idea
Lesion proves that each power has its own brain location.

After assigning each internal power a specific brain locus, Sanusi explains the empirical evidence for these assignments. The method of lesion—observing that damage to a particular area disrupts only one power—demonstrates that each power is specialized to its locus. This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the final part of the Arabic passage and its translation about lesion.
Focus on the cause-effect relationship.
2
Understand the Argument
Grasp the logic: if a lesion affects a locus, only the associated power's action is impaired, showing that power is tied to that locus.
This is a form of empirical proof.
3
Consider the Counterfactual
Think about what would happen if powers were not specialized: lesions would not produce localized effects.
The passage explicitly says so.
4
Connect to Modern Neurology
Reflect on how this ancient method anticipates modern localization of brain function.

Three Proofs that the Soul Perceives All Perceptions
Three proofs that the soul perceives all perceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational proofs from Islamic kalam that establish the rational soul as the perceiver of all perceptions, including particulars.

Core Idea
The soul is the one perceiver behind all sensory and intellectual experience.

This passage presents three concise proofs that the rational soul (النفس الناطقة) is the subject that perceives everything, from sensory particulars to universal concepts. The first proof is based on our ability to make universal judgments, the second on direct self-awareness, and the third on the soul's management of the body. Let's examine each one in turn.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's structure.
Notice that the passage begins by stating the claim and then gives three numbered reasons.
2
Understand the first proof: judgment of universals
The first proof argues that making judgments like 'Zayd is human' (universal on particular) or 'Zayd is not Amr' (particular negation) requires a single power that perceives both universals and particulars. This power cannot be corporeal, so it is the rational soul.
3
Understand the second proof: self-awareness
The second proof is experiential: you find yourself hearing, seeing, feeling hunger, and understanding concepts. The same 'I' perceives all these, so the perceiver is one — the soul.
This is a direct, undeniable experience.
4
Understand the third proof: management of the body
The soul manages the body, producing particular actions. To produce particular actions, it must know particulars, because a universal principle applies uniformly and cannot discriminate among individuals. Hence the soul perceives particulars.
The author also cites 'al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya' for support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة لأبحاث النوع الثاني وهي البحث الثالث: أكثر الكلام الذي نقلناه عنهم ( في ) إثبات ( هذه القوى ) وتعددها ( بعد ) بنائه على (نفي القادر المختار) الموجد لجميع الأشياء ابتداء بمجرد إرادته مبني ) على أن النفس ) الناطقة (ليست مدركة للجزئيات كما أشرنا إليه في أثناء الكلام المنقول (فلنتكلم في ذلك فنقول : المدرك لجميع أصناف الإدراكات ) هو ( النفس لوجوه : الأول : ما ذكرناه من الحكم بالكلي على الجزئي ) في مثل قولنا : زيد إنسان ) وبكل جزئي على أنه غير الآخر) أي والحكم بسلب أحد الجزأين عن الآخر كما في قولك : زيد ليس بعمرو، فلا بد من قوة تدرك الكليات وجميع أنواع الجزئيات من المحسوسات مشاهدة ومتخيلة والمعاني الجزئية متوهمة ومحفوظة ولا يجوز أن تكون هذه القوى جسمانية اتفاقاً فهي القوة العاقلة ( الثاني : وجداني بلا شبهة (إني واجد أسمع وأبصر وأجوع وأشبع وأدرك المعقولات فالمدرك للكل واحد وليس إلا النفس. ( الثالث : أن النفس مدبرة للبدن ( المعين ( فهو ) أي النفس بتأويل الإنسان (فاعل للجزئيات ) من الأفعال التدبيرية ( ولا بد له فيه أي في كونه فاعلاً للأفعال الجزئية ) من إدراك الجزئيات ( الصادرة عنه ( إذ الرأي الكلي نسبته إلى الكل من آحاد ذلك الكلي ( واحد فلا يصلح الرأي الكلي ( لكونه مصدراً للبعض دون البعض) فالنفس مدركة للجزئيات. وفي المباحث المشرقية : هي مدبرة لبدن شخص وتدبير الشيء للشخص من حيث هو ذلك الشخص يستحيل إلا بعد العلم به من حيث هو هو فإذن هي مدركة للبدن الجزئي
Conclusion of the investigations of the second type, and it is the third research: Most of the speech that we have transmitted from them concerning the proof of these powers and their multiplicity, after its foundation upon the denial of the free agent who originates all things initially by His mere will, is based upon the premise that the rational soul is not a perceiver of particulars, as we have indicated in the course of the transmitted speech. So let us speak on that, and we say: The perceiver of all kinds of perceptions is the soul, for reasons: The first: What we have mentioned of the judgment of the universal upon the particular, like our saying 'Zayd is human', and the judgment of each particular that it is other than another, i.e., the judgment of negating one of the two parts from the other, as in your saying 'Zayd is not 'Amr'. So there must be a power that perceives universals and all kinds of particulars from sensory objects, observed and imagined, and the particular meanings, imagined and preserved. And it is not possible for these powers to be corporeal, by agreement; so it is the rational power. The second: It is experiential, without doubt. I find myself hearing, seeing, hungry, full, and perceiving intelligibles; so the perceiver of everything is one, and it is none but the soul. The third: The soul is the manager of the designated body. So it (i.e., the soul, by interpretation 'the human') is an agent of the particular actions of management. And it is necessary for it, i.e., in its being an agent of particular actions, to have perception of the particulars that issue from it, for the universal opinion is one in its relation to all individuals of that universal, so the universal opinion is not suitable to be a source for some without others. So the soul perceives particulars. And in the Eastern Investigations: It is the manager of the body of a person, and the management of a thing for the person in terms of it being that person is impossible except after knowledge of it in terms of it being it. So then it is a perceiver of the particular body.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a set of three proofs that the soul (النفس) is the perceiver of all types of perceptions, including particulars. The author begins by noting that the previous discussion about the internal powers was based on a denial of God's free agency, but now he will argue directly. First proof: The human mind makes judgments involving universals and particulars, such as 'Zayd is human' (universal on particular) and 'Zayd is not 'Amr' (particular negation of another). Such judgments require a single power that perceives both universals and all kinds of particulars (sensory, imagined, estimated, remembered). This power cannot be corporeal, so it is the rational soul. Second proof: It is a direct self-awareness (وجداني) — one finds oneself hearing, seeing, feeling hunger, etc., and also perceiving intelligibles. The perceiver of all these diverse perceptions must be one thing, and that is the soul. Third proof: The soul manages the body, producing particular actions of management. To produce particular actions, it must know those particulars, because a universal principle applies uniformly to all instances and cannot discriminate among individuals. Therefore the soul perceives particulars. An additional citation from 'al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya' reinforces that management of a particular body requires knowledge of that body as individual.

المدرك
الكلي
الجزئي
القوة العاقلة
وجداني
مدبرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The senses alone perceive particulars without the soul's involvement.
Correction: The author shows that the soul integrates all perceptions, including sensory particulars, through judgments and self-awareness.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof that the soul is the perceiver of all perceptions?
A) It judges universals
B) It is found experientially that the self hears, sees, etc.

D) It perceives intelligibles

Key Takeaway
The soul is the single perceiver of both universals and particulars, as proven by rational argument and direct experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The second proof is experiential: you find yourself hearing, seeing, hungry, etc. Have you ever stopped to consider that the same 'I' underlies all these experiences? How does this realization affect your sense of identity and your responsibility before Allah for what you perceive and do?
?
If the soul is the single perceiver of all perceptions, then your thoughts, desires, and sensations are all witnessed by this soul. Does this make you more mindful (murāqaba) of what you allow yourself to see, hear, and imagine?
?
The third proof says the soul manages the body and must know particulars to act. Your soul directs every movement of your limbs. Are you aware that your bodily actions are not just physical but emanate from your soul's management? How can this awareness elevate the intention behind your daily actions?

Objections to the Soul's Perception of Particulars and the Answer
Listing four objections and a unified answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four objections to the soul's perception of particulars and the Kalām response that the senses are instruments while the soul is the true perceiver.

Core Idea
The soul perceives particulars through bodily instruments; lesions affect instruments, not the soul.

In this passage, the opponent raises four arguments that appear to show that the soul cannot perceive particular material objects. Each objection relies on empirical observations or logical contradictions. The author responds with a unified explanation: the senses are mere instruments (آلات), and the soul perceives by attending to the impressions in these instruments. This preserves both the soul's immateriality and its role in perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the four objections
Carefully read each of the four objections presented by the opponent. Note how each appeals to different types of evidence: common sense, medical observation, spatial requirements, and divisibility.
The objections are numbered in the text: الأول, الثاني, الثالث, الرابع.
2
Examine the unified answer
The author's answer reinterprets the objections by distinguishing between instruments (آلات) and the real perceiver (النفس). Understand how this resolves each objection.
The key phrase is 'أن الحواس آلات والنفس هي المدركة'.
3
Reflect on the sufficiency argument
The author concludes that the opponent's arguments do not disprove the existence of internal powers. Consider why the specialization of organs is evidence for distinct powers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللخصم ) القائل بأن النفس لا تدرك الجزئيات ( وجوه : الأول : نعلم ضرورة أن إدراك المبصرات حاصل للبصر و إدراك ( الأصوات للسمع وعلى هذا إدراك سائر المحسوسات فإنه حاصل للحواس المخصوصة ( وإنكار ذلك مكابرة مصادمة للبديهة فلا يلتفت إليه . ( الثاني : آفة كل عضو) هو محل لقوة ( توجب آفة فعله ) الذي نسب إليه فلولا أنه فعله حقيقة لما كان كذلك، وهذا إنما يظهر في الحواس الظاهرة وأما في الباطنة فيستبان بالتجارب الطبية من أن الآفة متى حدثت في مقدم البطن الأول اختل الإحساس دون تخيل المحسوسات السابقة، ومتى حدثت في مؤخره اختل التخيل دون الإحساس، وهكذا الحال في سائر القوى الباطنة ( الثالث : إذا أدركنا الكرة الشخصية مثلا فلا بد له ) أي لإدراكنا إياها أن ترتسم في المدرك ) منا (صورتها المتصفة بمقدار مخصوص ووضع معين وحيز لازم لهما . ( ومن المحال ارتسام ما له وضع وحيز فيما لا وضع ولا حيز له ) أعني إن النفس المجردة بل لا بد أن يكون ارتسامه في قوة جسمانية . ( الرابع: إذا تصورنا مربعاً) مشخصاً على مقدار مخصوص ( مجنحاً بمربعين) مشخصين على وضع معين ( هكذا ) ( فإنا نميز بين المربعات الثلاثة ونشير إلى وضع كل من الآخر على معنى أين هو من صاحبه ) وأحد الجناحين عن يمين المجنح والآخر عن يساره ( فلو كان محله ) أي محل ارتسام هذا المتصور هو ( النفس لزم كونه أي كون هذا المحل الذي هو النفس ( منقسماً انقساماً في الكم وأنه باطل لأنها مجردة عن المادة ) فلا تقبل الانقسام المقداري . ( والجواب ) عن وجوه الخصم: (أن من ذلك ) الذي ذكره ( لا ينفي كون الحواس ،آلات والنفس هي المدركة) فترتسم الجزئيات تلك الآلات وتدركها النفس لملاحظتها في آلاتها فلا يلزم انقسام النفس ولا كونها ذات وضع وحيز وتكون آفة الفعل باختلال الآلات دون المدرك ويصح إسناد الإدراك إلى تلك الآلات وإن لم تكن مدركة حقيقة وهذا القدر) الذي لا ينفيه شبه الخصم ( كاف) للمستدل ( في إثبات القوى المذكورة إذ يعلم بالضرورة أنه لولا) اختصاص كل عضو من تلك الأعضاء (بقوة ) مخصوصة (لما اختص بكونه آلة لنوع من المدركات دون الآخر) وبذلك يثبت وجود القوى) وتعددها وهو
And for the opponent who says that the soul does not perceive particulars there are objections: The first: We know necessarily that the perception of visible things is obtained by sight, and the perception of sounds is for hearing, and likewise the perception of the rest of the sensible things is obtained by the specific senses. Denial of that is obstinacy conflicting with intuition, so it is not paid attention to. The second: The lesion of every organ that is the locus of a power causes a lesion of its action that is attributed to it, for if it were not its action in reality, it would not be so. And this appears in the external senses; as for the internal, it is known by medical experiments: when a lesion occurs in the front of the first ventricle, sensation is disordered without the imagining of previous sensations, and when it occurs in its rear, imagination is disordered without sensation, and so on for the rest of the internal powers. The third: If we perceive a particular sphere, for example, it is necessary for our perception of it that its image, characterized by a specific magnitude and a determinate position and a necessary space for them, be imprinted in the perceiver from us. And it is impossible for something having position and space to be imprinted in that which has no position and no space, i.e., the immaterial soul. Rather, its imprinting must be in a corporeal power. The fourth: If we conceive a particular square of a specific magnitude, flanked by two squares of a determinate position, like this, then we distinguish between the three squares and point to the position of each from the other in terms of where it is from its companion, one of the flanks to the right of the flanked one and the other to its left. So if its locus, i.e., the locus of the imprinting of this conceived thing, is the soul, then it would necessitate this locus—i.e., that this locus which is the soul—being divided into a quantitative division, and that is false because it is abstract from matter, so it does not accept quantitative division. And the answer to the objections of the opponent: That [what] he mentioned does not negate that the senses are instruments and the soul is the perceiver. So the particulars are imprinted in those instruments, and the soul perceives them by observing them in its instruments. So neither division of the soul nor its being possessed of position and space follows. And the lesion of action is by the disorder of the instruments, not the perceiver. And it is valid to attribute perception to those instruments even though they are not really perceivers. And this amount, which the opponent's specious argument does not negate, is sufficient for the proponent in proving the aforementioned powers, for it is known necessarily that were it not for the specialization of each of those organs with a specific power, it would not be specialized in being an instrument for a type of perceptions without another. And by that the existence of the powers and their multiplicity is proven, and it is.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents four objections to the claim that the soul perceives particulars, followed by a unified answer. The first objection: perception is clearly attributed to external senses (sight, hearing, etc.), so denying that is absurd. The second: lesions in specific brain areas disrupt specific functions, proving those functions belong to those organs, not the soul. The third: a perceived particular object has magnitude, position, and space, so its imprint requires a locus with those properties; the soul is immaterial and lacks them, so the imprint must be in a corporeal power. The fourth: in imagining a complex shape (a square flanked by two squares), we distinguish positions; if the locus were the soul, it would need to be quantitatively divided to hold multiple positions, but the soul is indivisible. The answer denies the premise that the organs are the real perceivers: instead, they are instruments (آلات) in which particulars are imprinted, and the soul perceives them by attending to those instruments. Thus lesions affect the instruments, not the soul, and it is permissible to attribute perception to the instruments figuratively. This suffices to prove the existence of the internal powers, as the specialization of organs shows they are distinct instruments.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer denies the role of the brain in perception.
Correction: The answer does not deny the brain's role; it reinterprets the brain as an instrument (آلة) through which the soul perceives.

The Soul Perceives Particulars: Proof and Defense
Proof that the soul perceives particulars, with objections and a general answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that the soul directly perceives particulars and how the objections are answered by clarifying the role of the senses as instruments.

Core Idea
The soul perceives particulars using the senses as instruments.

This passage tackles a key question in Islamic psychology: who or what truly perceives individual objects? The author argues that the soul itself is the perceiver, not the senses. He presents a proof, then addresses four objections from those who think perception belongs solely to bodily faculties. The answer hinges on a crucial distinction between the soul as the agent and the senses as its tools.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof (1st part)
Start with the opening lines: the universal judgment cannot apply to some individuals but not others, so the soul must perceive particulars directly. Also note the citation from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya about management of the body.
This is the core argument: management requires knowledge of the particular.
2
Examine the four objections
The opponent gives four reasons why the soul cannot perceive particulars: (1) senses are the known perceivers, (2) organ damage affects perception, (3) perceived forms have spatial attributes, (4) imagining a shape with parts implies divisibility. Read each carefully.
These objections are based on common sense and empirical observations.
3
Understand the comprehensive answer
The author answers all objections at once: the senses are merely instruments (alat); the soul is the true perceiver. Forms are imprinted in the instruments, and the soul attends to them. No division or spatial attributes are imposed on the soul.
This distinction between instrument and agent is crucial.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument matters for Islamic theology and psychology. It establishes the soul's independence from the body and its role as the subject of perception.

( الثاني : آفة كل عضو) هو محل لقوة ( توجب آفة فعله ) الذي نسب إليه فلولا أنه فعله حقيقة لما كان كذلك، وهذا إنما يظهر في الحواس الظاهرة وأما في الباطنة فيستبان بالتجارب الطبية من أن الآفة متى حدثت في مقدم البطن الأول اختل الإحساس دون تخيل المحسوسات السابقة، ومتى حدثت في مؤخره اختل التخيل دون الإحساس، وهكذا الحال في سائر القوى الباطنة ( الثالث : إذا أدركنا الكرة الشخصية مثلا فلا بد له ) أي لإدراكنا إياها أن ترتسم في المدرك ) منا (صورتها المتصفة بمقدار مخصوص ووضع معين وحيز لازم لهما . ( والجواب ) عن وجوه الخصم: (أن من ذلك ) الذي ذكره ( لا ينفي كون الحواس ،آلات والنفس هي المدركة) فترتسم الجزئيات تلك الآلات وتدركها النفس لملاحظتها في آلاتها فلا يلزم انقسام النفس ولا كونها ذات وضع وحيز وتكون آفة الفعل باختلال الآلات دون المدرك ويصح إسناد الإدراك إلى تلك الآلات وإن لم تكن مدركة حقيقة وهذا القدر) الذي لا ينفيه شبه الخصم ( كاف) للمستدل ( في إثبات القوى المذكورة إذ يعلم بالضرورة أنه لولا) اختصاص كل عضو من تلك الأعضاء (بقوة ) مخصوصة (لما اختص بكونه آلة لنوع من المدركات دون الآخر) وبذلك يثبت وجود القوى) وتعددها وهو المطلوب
The first observatory - the second type: the inner perceptive faculty. From the perception of particulars (emanating from it) for the universal judgment relates to all individuals of that universal (one) so the universal judgment is not suitable (for being a source for some to the exclusion of others) so the soul perceives particulars. And in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: it is the manager of a person's body and the management of a thing for a person insofar as he is that person is impossible except after knowledge of it insofar as it is itself, so then it perceives the particular body. (And for the opponent) who says that the soul does not perceive particulars (are aspects): First: we know necessarily that the perception of visible things occurs by sight and the perception of sounds by hearing and likewise the perception of other sensible things occurs by the specific senses, and denying that is obstinacy contradicting intuition so it is not heeded. Second: the injury of each organ is a locus for a power (that necessitates injury to its act) which is attributed to it, for if it were not truly its act it would not be so, and this is apparent in the external senses; as for the internal, it becomes clear through medical experiments: when injury occurs in the front of the first ventricle, sensation is impaired without imagination of previous sensibles, and when it occurs in the back, imagination is impaired without sensation, and so on for the other inner faculties. Third: when we perceive a particular sphere, for example, it is necessary for (our perception of it) that its form—characterized by a specific quantity and definite position and a space necessary for them—be imprinted in the perceiver (in us). And it is impossible for that which has position and space to be imprinted in that which has no position nor space, I mean the abstract soul; rather its imprinting must be in a corporeal faculty. Fourth: when we imagine a specific square of a specific quantity, winged by two specific squares in a definite position, like this, we distinguish between the three squares and point to the position of each relative to the other in the sense of where it is in relation to its companion—one wing to the right of the winged and the other to its left. So if its locus, i.e., the locus of the imprinting of this imagined thing, were the soul, it would necessitate that the soul be divided into quantitative division, which is false because it is abstract from matter, so it does not accept quantitative division. (And the answer) to the opponent's aspects: (that from that) which he mentioned (does not negate that the senses are instruments and the soul is the perceiver); the particulars are imprinted in those instruments and the soul perceives them by attending to them in their instruments, so neither division of the soul nor its being possessed of position and space is entailed, and injury to the act occurs by the disruption of the instruments, not the perceiver, and it is valid to attribute perception to those instruments even if they are not truly perceivers. And this amount (which the opponent's doubt does not negate is sufficient) for the arguer (to prove the mentioned faculties, for it is known necessarily that were it not for) the specialization of each of those organs (with a specific power) it would not be specialized as an instrument for a type of perceived things to the exclusion of another (and by that the existence of the faculties is proven) and their multiplicity, and that is the goal.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the soul (al-nafs) perceives particulars (al-juz'iyyat), followed by four objections from an opponent who denies this, and a comprehensive answer. The author begins by stating his own argument: the universal judgment (al-ra'y al-kulli) relates to all individuals of a universal, so it cannot be a source for some to the exclusion of others; therefore the soul must perceive particulars directly. He then cites the work al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: the soul manages a specific body, and management requires knowledge of that body as a particular, so the soul perceives the particular body. The opponent raises four objections: (1) perception of sensible things is known to be from the senses, and denying this is stubborn; (2) damage to an organ impairs its function, proving the organ is the agent; (3) a perceived object's form has quantity and position, so it cannot be in a non-spatial soul; (4) imagining a specific shape with parts in different positions indicates divisibility, so the imagination must be in a divisible organ. The answer to all objections is that they do not disprove that the senses are mere instruments (alat) and the soul is the true perceiver. The forms are imprinted in the instruments, and the soul perceives by attending to them; thus no division or spatiality is required. Organ damage affects the instrument, not the perceiver, so perception can be attributed to the instruments in a metaphorical sense. This suffices to prove the existence of multiple inner faculties, because each organ is specialized for a type of perception by its specific power.

Classification of Active Faculties: Inciting and Motor
Classification of active faculties into inciting and motor, with four principles of voluntary action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of active faculties into inciting and motor, the subdivision of inciting into appetitive and irascible, and the four principles of voluntary action: conception, desire, will, and motor execution.

Core Idea
Voluntary action involves four sequential principles: conception, desire, will, and motor execution.

This passage from al-Mawaqif examines the active faculties that produce movement. The author distinguishes between the inciting power that prompts action and the motor power that executes it, and then unpacks the psychological process behind every voluntary act. By tracing the sequence from conception to physical movement, we gain insight into the integrated nature of human action.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay attention to the definitions and divisions.
2
Identify the two main types of active faculties
Notice the division into inciting (ba'itha) and motor (muharrika). The inciting is further divided into appetitive and irascible.
3
Trace the four principles of voluntary action
The passage lists conception, desire, will, and motor execution. Understand how they are arranged sequentially.
4
Reflect on the benefit of the term 'active'
The commentary explains why the author prefers 'active' over 'motor'.
5
Review the lexical notes
Understand the meanings of 'nazu'a' and 'shanj' as given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(النوع الثالث : القوى الفاعلة) هي التي عبر عنها فيما سبق بالمحركة على معنى أن لها مدخلاً في الحركة إما بالتحريك أو الإعانة على قياس ما مر في المدركة وفائدة العدول ظاهرة ( وتنقسم إلى ( قوة ( باعثة ) على الحركة (و) قوة ( محركة) مباشرة للتحريك . ( أما الباعثة ) وتسمى شوقية ونزوعة ( فإما لجلب النفع وتسمى شهوية وإما لدفع الضرر وتسمى غضبية. وأما المحركة فهي التي تمدد الأعصاب ) بتشنيج العضلات ( فتقرب الأعضاء إلى مباديها كما في قبض اليد ) مثلاً ( وترخيها ) أي ترخي الأعصاب بإرخاء العضلات ( فتبعد الأعضاء عن مبادئها كما في البسط ) أي بسط اليد، وهذه القوة ) المنبتة في العضلات ( هي المبدأ القريب ( للحركة والمبدأ البعيد ) هو ( التصور وبينهما الشوق والإرادة) فهذه مبادئ أربعة مترتبة للأفعال الاختيارية الصادرة عن الحيوان ( فإن النفس تتصور الحركة) أولاً، ( فتشتاق إليها ) ثانياً، بناءً على اعتقاد نفع فيها ( فتريدها ) ثالثاً، (إرادة قصد ) إليها ( وإيجاد لها فتحصل الحركة بتمديد الأعصاب وإرخائها رابعاً، وقال بعضهم: الشوق إنما يوجد فيمن ليس قدرته تامة فتردد وتشتاق وأما الذي يثق بقدرته فلا شوق له . قوله : ( وفائدة العدول ظاهرة ولعل فائدة العدول هي التنبيه على أن تلك القوى كما سميت محركة سميت أيضاً فاعلة والأولى من ذلك أن يقال الفاعلية كما تتناول أيضاً نفيس الأخذ والبطش والقبض والبسط مثلا يترتب على المحركية والمحركية لا تتناول نفس الأخذ والبطش ونحوهما فتكون الفاعلية أشمل من المحركة. وقوله : نزوعة يقال : نزع إلى أهله أي اشتاق . وقوله : بتشنيج العضلات الشنج بفتح الشين المعجمة وسكون النون هو تقبض في الجلد وشنجه تشنيجاً كذا في الصحاح.
(The third type: the active faculties) are those which were previously called motor faculties, meaning that they have an involvement in movement either by moving or by assisting, analogous to what was mentioned regarding the perceptive faculty, and the benefit of the alteration is clear. (And they are divided into) a power (inciting) to movement (and) a power (motor) directly causing movement. (As for the inciting) and it is called desiderative (shawqiyya) and appetitive (nazu'a): it is either for attracting benefit and is called appetitive (shahwiyya) or for repelling harm and is called irascible (ghadabiyya). (As for the motor) it is that which expands the nerves by contracting the muscles and brings the limbs closer to their origins as in closing the hand, for example, and relaxes them, i.e., relaxes the nerves by relaxing the muscles, so it distances the limbs from their origins as in opening, i.e., opening the hand. And this power (embedded in the muscles) is the proximate principle (of movement, while the remote principle) is (conception, and between them is desire and will). So these are four principles arranged for voluntary actions emanating from the animal. (For the soul conceives the movement) firstly, (then desires it) secondly, based on a belief of benefit in it, (then wills it) thirdly, (a will of intention) towards it (and bringing it into existence, so the movement occurs by expansion and relaxation of the nerves) fourthly. And some said: desire occurs only in one whose power is not complete, so he hesitates and desires, but one who is confident in his power has no desire. His words: (And the benefit of the alteration is clear. Perhaps the benefit of the alteration is to indicate that those faculties, just as they are called motor, are also called active, and better than that is to say: activity (al-fa'iliyya) as it also includes the very act of grasping, seizing, clenching, and opening, for example, which follow from motricity, while motricity does not include the very act of grasping, seizing, and the like; so activity is more comprehensive than motricity. And his words: 'nazu'a' is said: nazi'a ila ahlihi, meaning he yearned. And his words: 'by contracting the muscles': al-shanj, with fatha on the shin and sukun on the nun, is a contraction in the skin; and shanajahu tashnijan, thus in al-Sihah.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of faculties: the active faculties (al-quwwa al-fa'ila). The author notes that these were previously called motor faculties (al-muharrika), but he uses 'active' because they have a role in movement either directly or indirectly, and the benefit of the change is later explained. He then divides them into two kinds: (1) the inciting faculty (al-ba'itha), also called the desiderative (shawqiyya) or appetitive (nazu'a), which is further subdivided into the appetitive (shahwiyya) for seeking benefit and the irascible (ghadabiyya) for repelling harm; and (2) the motor faculty (al-muharrika) which directly causes movement by expanding and contracting nerves and muscles, bringing limbs toward or away from their origins, as in closing and opening the hand. The motor faculty is the proximate principle of movement, while the remote principle is conception (tasawwur), with desire (shawq) and will (irada) between them. These four principles—conception, desire, will, and motor execution—are arranged sequentially in voluntary actions: the soul first conceives the movement, then desires it based on a belief of benefit, then wills it with an intention, and finally the movement occurs through nerve expansion and relaxation. A note is added that some say desire only occurs when one's power is incomplete, leading to hesitation and desire, whereas one confident in his power has no desire. The passage concludes with a commentator's gloss: the benefit of calling them 'active' rather than 'motor' is that 'active' (fa'ila) includes the actual acts of grasping, seizing, etc., which follow from motricity, while 'motor' does not; thus 'active' is more comprehensive. Additionally, lexical explanations are given for 'nazu'a' (yearning) and 'shanj' (contraction) with a reference to the dictionary al-Sihah.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the motor faculty alone causes movement.
Correction: The passage shows that movement is preceded by conception, desire, and will, which are necessary principles.

Checkpoint

A) Conception (tasawwur)

Key Takeaway
Voluntary action arises from four sequential principles: conception, desire, will, and motor execution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you ever pause to examine whether your desire is truly for benefit or merely a fleeting impulse? How often do you act without the first principle of conception, simply out of habit or whim? This lesson calls you to be conscious of the sequence of your voluntary actions. Will you commit to pausing before acting to ensure your actions are grounded in true conception and noble desire?
?
The source mentions that some scholars say desire is absent in one who is confident in his power. Do you live with such confidence in Allah's power that you act without hesitation or desire for worldly gain? Or are your actions still driven by restless desire because your reliance is on your own incomplete power? Examine your heart: are your actions marked by trust in Allah or by anxious desire?

The Theoretical and Practical Powers of the Rational Soul
Defining the rational soul and its two powers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the theoretical and practical powers of the rational human soul, their functions, and how they interact in human cognition and action.

Core Idea
The rational soul has two powers: theoretical for universals, practical for particulars.

In this passage, Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a foundational classification of the rational soul's powers. It distinguishes between the theoretical power, which grasps universal truths, and the practical power, which guides particular actions. The text also explains how they are related, how the practical power derives from the theoretical through syllogistic reasoning, and how this process gives rise to human emotions. This framework is essential for understanding Islamic philosophical psychology.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage with translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Don't worry about details yet.
2
Identify the two powers
Locate where the text defines the theoretical power (al-quwwa al-nazariyya) and the practical power (al-quwwa al-‘amaliyya). Notice their respective functions.
The theoretical power deals with universal judgments; the practical power deals with particular actions.
3
Understand their relationship
The text states that the practical power derives from the theoretical. Find the explanation of how this occurs through reflection and syllogism.
The example of the syllogism with a universal major premise and a particular minor premise is key.
4
Note the emotional outcomes
The passage describes how the practical power's judgments lead to emotional states like laughter, shame, and fear. Observe the causal chain.
These emotions are specific to humans because of the rational soul.
5
Reflect on human uniqueness
Consider why these two powers are said to be unique to humans among animals. This ties into the broader theme of human distinctiveness.

The third section: the rational human soul. It is among the three sections in the third chapter which is devoted to explaining the composites that have (in the human soul, i.e., in explaining its powers), and therefore he said: 'and its powers,' meaning those specific to it. It is called the rational power. By virtue of its perception of universals and judgment between them with affirmative or negative relation, it is called the theoretical power and the theoretical intellect. And by virtue of its derivation of intellectual crafts and its practice of opinion and deliberation in particular matters concerning what should be done or left, it is called the practical power and the practical intellect. These are two powers that are distinct either in essence or in consideration, which humans are distinguished by among all animals. The first is for universal judgments, whether true or false, and the second is for judgments concerning particular actions, whether they are goods or evils, beautiful or ugly. This power is derived from the theoretical power, because extracting particular opinions occurs only through a form of reflection and syllogism; there must be a universal premise. For example, one says: this action is such and such, and everything that is such is beautiful (should be done) or ugly (should be left). Then the minor premise of the syllogism is particular and its major is universal, and from them an opinion is obtained about a particular future matter among possible things. For necessary and impossible things are not deliberated about in how to bring them into existence or annihilate them, nor are past and present deliberated about for bringing into existence or annihilating; rather, that is specific to future matters. When this power judges this particular opinion, its judgment is followed by the movement of the social power to move the body, and there occur in it (i.e., the rational human soul) from the practical desiderative power passive states which are followed by bodily conditions, which are laughter (which follows wonder occurring in the soul from perception of strange things whose causes are hidden), and shame, modesty, and their sisters, such as fear, grief, hatred, and other emotions specific to humans. Thus it becomes evident that the soul is affected by its powers just as it affects them. His saying: (The third section among the three sections, etc.) Do not be unaware that the author mentioned in the first observatory of this station on substance two chapters, and mentioned in the second of them five sections, and mentioned in the fourth of them three chapters, and mentioned in the third of them also three sections. So the third section that he mentions here is the third of these three sections, and the fifth section that he mentions after this is the fifth of those five sections. And his saying: 'distinguished by' — the letter bā here enters upon the restricted [term] not the restricting [term]. And his saying: 'are not deliberated' — 't-tarawwī' on the measure of 't-tarqī' meaning reflection.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage defines the third section of the third chapter concerning the rational human soul and its powers. It begins by stating that this section is part of three sections in the chapter that explains composites in the human soul, i.e., its powers. The rational power is named such because it perceives universals and judges between them. It then distinguishes two powers: the theoretical power (al-quwwa al-nazariyya) which deals with universal judgments and is also called the theoretical intellect (al-‘aql al-nazari); and the practical power (al-quwwa al-‘amaliyya) which derives intellectual crafts and engages in deliberation about particular actions. These two powers are distinct either in essence or in consideration and are unique to humans. The theoretical power handles universal judgments whether true or false, while the practical power handles judgments about particular actions whether good or evil. The practical power is derived from the theoretical because extracting particular opinions requires reflection and syllogism involving a universal premise. The example given is a syllogism: this action is such, and everything such is beautiful (should be done) or ugly (should be left). The minor premise is particular, the major is universal, producing an opinion about a future possible matter. Necessary and impossible things are not deliberated, nor past or present. When the practical power judges, the social power moves the body, resulting in emotional states like laughter, shame, modesty, etc. The passage ends with a commentator's note clarifying the book's structure and linguistic points about the word 't-tarawwī'.

Correction: The passage states they are two powers distinct either in essence or in consideration, and the practical power derives from the theoretical, but they are not the same.

Key Takeaway
The rational soul's theoretical and practical powers are distinct yet complementary, enabling human cognition and moral action through syllogistic reasoning and giving rise to uniquely human emotions.

The Philosophers' Materialistic View of Bodies
Natural phenomena explained by philosophers' materialistic framework.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' explanation of natural phenomena and bodily differences as purely material processes, denying the role of a freely acting Creator.

Core Idea
Philosophers explain all bodily change through matter and motion, not divine action.

The text begins by describing natural phenomena such as earthquakes and fires as physical processes, then summarizes that these are the views of philosophers who deny the existence of a creator who freely chooses. This lesson examines their materialistic framework and its contrast with theological explanations.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of natural phenomena
Read the Arabic passage describing vapor, earthquakes, and fires. Notice how each phenomenon is explained by natural causes like heat, moisture, and motion.
The details show the philosophers' commitment to natural causation.
2
Identify the summary statement
Find where the text says 'All that we mentioned... are the views of the philosophers.' This ties the natural descriptions to their denial of al-Qādir al-Mukhtār.
3
Analyze the chain of explanation
Trace how philosophers attribute differences in bodies to preparedness in matter, then to forms, then to celestial motions. This is their complete causal chain.
This chain excludes any direct divine intervention.
